Porsches (in fact all cars) in Germany are very expensive.  MUCH MUCH
more expensive than they are here.  This is a direct result of
unification.  This has begun to spread to neighboring countries.
Europe is not a good place to go hunting for value in used cars right
now. 

According to the Illustrated Porsche Buyers Guide, and verified by my
own experience, there are a higher concentration of Porsches in
California than Germany.  The IPBG claims that, at least up to the
time it was published (1986, I think) half of all Porsches ever sold
were sold in the US, and half of those were sold in southern
california.

Plus, southern californian weather leaves the cars in better shape
than European weather.

Moral: looking for a used Porsche?  go to california.

=====

The star heads are stock - someone did you a favor and replaced half of them
with the allen head bolts.  I have the correct star socket and still the damn
things strip out.  Then I get out the pipe wrench - that works great, so you
don't need the grinder.
   A word of warning - do _not_ over tighten those bolts, as I did a couple
of times with the pipe wrench.  It overstresses them and then one day when
you put away from a stop sign you shear the bolts.  All 4 of them.  And then
you are up the proverbial creek.  Last time it happened to me (before I got
smart and started using loctite) I was lucky enough to have a reversible,
rechargable drill and a set of bolt extracting bits with me...still took me
several hours, though.  I always keep a couple of extra bolts in the trunk,
also.
   Fortunately, the 911 has the allen head bolts stock, so I dont't have that
problem anymore.

- Jeff Shorey

I can only speak for Germany, not all of Europe. I lived in Germany in '86 and
 '87, more precisely, in Zuffenhausen, about 1 km from the factory. While there
are quite a few Porsches (particularly 911's) around Stuttgart, most of which
are from recent vintage, finding a good used one is impossible. They get DRIVEN 
over there. Personally, I went through a set of rear tires every 10K kms (6.2k
miles). Driving flat-out is part of my  daily  commute, which sometime include 
a lap or two around the old Solitude Ring on the way home, if I need to wind
down.

    > Porsches (in fact all cars) in Germany are very expensive.  MUCH MUCH
    > more expensive than they are here.  This is a direct result of
    > unification.  This has begun to spread to neighboring countries.

Perhaps, but how many people in the East can afford Porsches? For Americans, I
think it has more to do with the lousy exchange rates. I bought my 83 SC from
an american army doctor, for US$17K early in '86 because it was a better deal
than I could find on the economy, the exchange rate at the time was 2.5 dm=$1.

When  I left in 87, I bought my 85 Carrera, again from an American for US$22K, 
they were going for around 50K dms at the time.

The current exchange rate hovers around 1.55 DM = $1. You can do your own
arithmetic.

I imported my car back to the US, it is a piece of cake, here's the break down
of cost:

- Shipping to Baltimore - $700 (company paid)
- Marine insurance, port fee, triple bonding - $700
- import duty 2.72% - I sweet talked my way out of it. I still CAN'T believe
  it.
- EPA and DOT costs including ALL the paper works to the DOT folks and the
conversion works - $3600.

The conversion was done by Champagne Imports in Hatfield, PA. I visited them in
advanced, they are a good, reputable outfit, who is still in business, though I
can't imagine doing much conversion work anymore.

    > Moral: looking for a used Porsc> he?  go to california.

Buy one (or several) in California, and ship them to Germany, you can make
some money, my friends are doing it. The buyers will have to get TUEV approval,
which is a piece of cake and pay the mehrwertsteuer (VAT) of 14%, which again 
can be *adjusted*.

-------
Some personal observations:

1. Why do most people think it is much harder to import grey market cars?

They probably read too much and got caught in the media hysterics, caused
mainly by the greedy Mercedes Benz of North America folks. The difference is,
the government now requires a bond trippling the value of the car instead of
equipvalent. AND, there is no longer a 5 years EPA, waiver rule.  All cars have
after '68, have to comply to the equipvalent US models.

2. What is the difference between euro and US 911?

EPA: most (all?) euro 911 (Porsches) now have catalyst. If it ain't there, you
got to add it.

DOT: 

- There is no difference in bumper height (as someone suggested), unless it
has been privately lowered. 

- Most 911 (Porsches) since 83 have door beams for all markets. Both of mine
did. Bumpers have no shock absorbers, for 911, you can buy them and retrofit 
yourself. 

- Markers, lights, speedo, Fuel funnel etc have to be added. The euro H4 are 
a zillion times better than the US lights (9004 included).

Performance:

- The euro SC's was rated as followed: 180ps in 78, 188ps in 79,80, 204 ps for
81-83. Mine 83 SC had NO problems keeping up with my friends Porsches. most of
my friends works for Porsche Styling or the racing department, they are not
timid drivers. I remembered the first time driving home after the inaugural
Super Cup race at Nuerburgring, I passed one of my friend's 944 Turbo on
autobahn 8, no one believed they got passed by an SC going flat out. ALL
PORsches are different, the ratings are nominal.

- The Carrera had 231 PS, it had 11:1 compression, different ECU, and forged 
piston instead of the heavier and less durable Kelsey-hayes alunimum pistons.
I saw 260+ kpm on the speedo and redline in 5th on the tach a few times. I know
there is some error in the speedo, but not the tach.


- Steve.

From: dorr@hyperion.llnl.gov (Milo Dorr)
Message-Id: <9011020940.AA20041@hyperion.llnl.gov>
To: 0003844665@mcimail.com
Subject: RE: CV Bolts
Cc: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu

>What size star-allen do I need?  Are there hidden circlips, as on Rabbits?  Is
>the outer one hard to do (1986 with aluminum trailing arms)?  Is Loctite
>necessary if the bolts are torqued correctly?  Are the 930 allen-bolts an exact
>fit?

I have rebuilt the half-shafts on my '86 951 as well as on the '84 944
I owned before that.  You need an 8mm 12-point star-allen socket to
remove and install the CV bolts.  Automotion (1-(800)-777-8881) sells
such a widget (for a standard 3/8" rachet) for $16.95, but I found an
8mm 12-point bit (to use in a standard socket) at a local hardware
store for a few dollars.

I did not encounter any particular difficulties removing the CV bolts,
except for one bolt that I believe was stripped (the head, not the
threads) by the previous owner or his mechanic.  The only circlip
(which you need some patience and a good sturdy set of snap ring
pliers to deal with) is the one that holds the CV joint on the shaft.
The aluminum control arms did not pose any particular problem.  On
both the inner and outer joints you can only reach a couple of bolts
without turning the axle because of the angle the axle makes with the
joints, particularly with the suspension in full "droop".

After inspecting the joints for scoring, I used Swepco Moly-Lube to
repack them.  I did not use Loctite on reassembly, but simply torqued
the bolts to 42 Nm (31 ft-lb) as prescribed in the factory manual.  I
don't know if 930 bolts will fit.  Don't forget to notice which way
the joints are facing before you remove them so that you can reinstall
them the same way.  There is a groove etched on the outside of the
joint that should be nearest the side facing away from the shaft.

By the way, if you've never tried to reassemble a CV joint before,
you're in for some fun (if you like puzzles, that is).  There are two
ways it can go back together.  As usual in life, the wrong way is the
easy one.  You'll know when this has occured, however, because the
inner and outer races will be securely locked together!

Good luck,

-Milo

}RE 944 CV joints:
 
They are EXACTLY the same as doing the rabbit inners, only same on both
ends.  6mm I beleive.  The CV Kits come with new bolts (at least that's
how I get them from the wholesaler) a new boot and a tube of CV grease.
Cost is in the $70 range per.  I replaced them one at a time, over a
span of about 2 mos, so I would at lease inspect all if you are going to
bother, and repack.  Best to opt for replacement.  You have to pull the
whole axel anyway so at least replace them in pairs.

The 930 boltas are about 2 mm longer, which makes no diff since all
holes are drilled all the way through flanges.  Mine were torqued to
feel.  I have a whole mess of "cheesehead" screws that are brand new if
anyone needs some, and it's not a bad idea to carry a few spares.

To replace you will need a 4" gear puller to get the inner "race" off
the axel.  It is held on with a single non-holed circlip.  No fun to get
that off.

In any event, my 951 -w- 70K miles needed all 4. Look for spalling on
the races.  They wear MUCH faster if the car has been lowered alot.

Steve

} Yes, the "triple square" bolts are all over my 944S: the clutch, the rear axle,
} the rear hatch hardware, etc.  Anyone know of a reasonable source for the tools?
Porsche calls these "cheesehead bolts"

You should pick up a 6mm, 8mm, 10mm, 12mm.  Most are 6mm, clutch, CVs,
hatch hardware.

The local pep boys sells them. They are made by Leslie and almost any
good car parts store should sell them.  They come bubble wrapped.

You can buy them with or without the socket.  The big mothers (10mm and
12mm) come as a shaft only.  The 12mm is needed to pull the tranny
tailshaft (Fun fun fun!) and for the flywheel bolts (also a barrel of
laughs).

I think the only parts of the turbo which I havn't disassembled are the
rear wheel bearings.  Note to follow on that one.

If you can't get them in Jersey (since they may be restricted to real
states ;^}  ) I would be happy to pick one up and bring to a track
event.

Steve

  I got my "triple-square" from an import car parts shop that specialized
in bug parts.  Evidently the bug has the same format of bolts (isn't that
reassuring).  Note that this is also the same kind of bolt used in the 911
door striker bolts, and I have seen them in the door hinge bolts, although
I don't know if that is stock.  Perhaps Porsche went to all the trouble of
using this bolt to impose a sort of a mandatory torque limit on the bolt, ie
it will strip out by design if you torque it too hard.
   Also, don't be fooled - this is not a "torx" head bolt.  A torx key/socket
will work, but not very well.  I spent a couple of years with one until I
found the correct tool in the above mentioned bug store.

   Heh, I know about the CV puzzle - they are a bitch to get apart once you
put them together wrong.  An easy way to put them together properly is to
remember that the wide spacing on the outside race goes with the narrow
spacing on the inside race.  The spacing between the bearings that is.  Use
a good moly grease (I think Swepco 101 is the right kind, Tweeks sells it) and
get a new boot if the one you have looks old.  It's very messy when a boot
breaks open, and very expensive if you don't catch it in time.

- Jeff Shorey

	Questions:
	
	    1. do I need to use any sealant on or around the pushrod tube
	       seals?  i don't really want them to leak any more.

Yes, you need to use permatex 98H. It's kind of burgundy colored goop
that seals hot engine joints very well. It *will* leak oil if you 
don't use something, and people I respect use Permatex 98H. (Hi Dave! ;^}) 
	
	    2. will it be difficult to put the rocker assemblies back on?
	       will I have to compress the valve springs?  i don't have
	       a spring compressor tool...

No, it's not difficult at all, but pay close attention to it!
You shouldn't need a spring compressor, some of the valve springs will 
compress on their own as you torque it down. Make sure, though, that 
all the pushrods are in their seats at both ends as you bring the rocker
down. Torque it down nice and slow in stages. Your shop manual will 
have the final torque value and a procedure outlining the torque stages
on the way....
	
	    3. will i need to do a valve adjustment afterward?  i look
	       forward to it being much easier than the bucket & shim
	       valves on my Audi...  What's the easiest way to turn the
	       engine over to line up the valves?  I don't want to push
	       the car because it'll be up on stands...
	
Well, a pure technical theory would say no, but I always do. It is 
such an easy process that there is no reason not to. Move your 
motor with a large metric crescent wrench on the pulley nut.
Don't use an SAE one or it may round off your pulley nut. 
Get the motor to TDC on #1 cylinder, as indicated by the position
of the rotor (you take the distributor cap off). Then turn the 
motor  *backwards* (that's counter-clockwise) and you can do the 
cylinders in order (1,2,3,4). As you have guessed, the firing 
order running forwards is 1,4,3,2.  This has the added advantage 
that you do the 1/2 side and then the 3/4 side. 

If you don't have a book that describes the process, How To Rebuild
Your VOLKSWAGEN Air-Cooled Engine by Tom Wilson (HP Books) gives
full and specific information for the type 4/914 motor. I highly 
recommend this book. It's $12.95 well spent at the Bugformance shop
on El Camino Real in Sunnyvale. 

	Anything else I should beware of?
	
Yeah, use new pushrod tubes. They're cheap.
And use all new gaskets when you put it back together.
I don't use the 98H on the valve cover gaskets, they
don't generally leak at all. But those who are really
conscientious sometimes use Copper Gasket Coat spray
on those to keep them well sealed.

If you get stuck, give me a call at (408) 336-3382, I should
be at home all weekend. Seriously. 

JZ

p.s. The bit about the SAE crescent wrench is a joke. :^}

Date: Fri, 2 Nov 90 20:06:25 est
From: Jeffrey A. Shorey <js@dukee.egr.duke.edu>
Message-Id: <9011030106.AA13462@dukee.egr.duke.edu>
To: merithew@jessica.stanford.edu, porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Re:  pushrod tube seal replacement on 914

   I've never done this to a 914, but my roommate from Texas just
did it to his car and told me about it...

>1)  remove the rocker assemblies (the two rods around which the
>    rockers pivot)
>
Do you really need to remove the entire rocker assembly?  Can you
just take out the adjustment screw and get the pushrod out?  My
roommate didn't say anything about this...

>2)  remove the strange thin-wire clip that (apparently) holds the
>    tubes in place (?)
>
The pushrod retaining wire...

>3)  pull out the pushrod tubes & seals, put on new seals, and
>    slide the tubes back in.
>
Replace the tubes if you can - they are designed for a one-time
installation and crush in place.  Also, I have heard about some
super-duper seals that do a lot better job than stock.  Lube the
seals with silicone paste or a grease-type lubricant.  Dow-Corning
111 compound works well here (Borrowing from Bruce Anderson's
Performance Handbook).
>4)  put the thin-wire clip back in and put the rocker assemblies
>    back on.
>
>Questions:
>
>    1. do I need to use any sealant on or around the pushrod tube
>       seals?  i don't really want them to leak any more.
>
Yes - see above.
>    2. will it be difficult to put the rocker assemblies back on?
>       will I have to compress the valve springs?  i don't have
>       a spring compressor tool...
>
If you have to remove them, you won't have to compress the springs.
Also, I think the rocker assemblies are held in place with locknuts.
Use new ones for this - I had a set loosen up on me in Colorado one
year and had to drive back to Texas with 3 cylinders - it sucks only
being able to go 65mph.
>    3. will i need to do a valve adjustment afterward?  i look
>       forward to it being much easier than the bucket & shim
>       valves on my Audi...  What's the easiest way to turn the
>       engine over to line up the valves?  I don't want to push
>       the car because it'll be up on stands...
>
Yes, you will have to adjust the valves if you loosened or remove the
adjustment screws - good idea to do it anyway while you have the stupid
covers off.  The way I turn the engine is to jack up one side and have
the other side on the ground (both rear tires mounted).  Release the
parking break (make sure the car is stable) and put it in 5th gear.
Take off the distributor cap to see which cylinder is on the firing
stroke - the rotor will point to the appropriate ignition wire.  Two
of the cylinders fire on TDC, which is marked on the impeller with an
O.  I don't think 180 degrees is marked - you'll have to guess.  At TDC
or 180 degrees, the cylinder pointed to by the rotor will be the one
you want to adjust, as both valves are fully closed.
The cylinders fire 180 degrees apart in an order I forget.  Just turn
the raised wheel to rotate the crank until the appropriate marking comes
into view.  Oh, I forgot - you need a mirror to see the impeller through
the sight hole as the markings are on the side toward the rear of the car.
Not very convenient, unless some nice person painted the timing markings on
the front of the impeller, hint, hint.
   Adjust both the intake and exhaust valves to .006" or .15mm.  Sometimes
in really hot weather I move the exhaust valves out a little more,
sacrificing performance for engine safety.  You need a 13mm wrench and
a stubby screwdriver - set the adjusting screw so that your feeler gauge
just drags in the gap and lock the nut while keeping the screw from
turning with the screwdriver.  If the engine is in the car, I don't think
you can adjust the front passenger side cylinder because a suspension mount
is in the way - you'll have to wait until you drop the engine to do that one.
Unfortunately that is also the same one that is under the oil cooler and
is notorious in Bugs for overheating.
   My description is a bit unorganized, but it should contain all the
pertinent information - use the Heinous (Haynes) manual as a reference.

Have fun!!!

- Jeff Shorey

Craig,

I empathize with your uncertainty about the tensioning values.  After
one has purchased a very delicate (therefore very expense) instrument
to measure the tension in our precious belts, it is rather
disconcerting to see such a wide range of suggested tension values
(although the 1.6-2.0 value you reported is a new low for me, and
sends chills up my spine).  Here is what my factory manual (which, if
you don't have, you should.  After all, it costs less than half what
the P9201 tool cost :-)) indicates:

Cam belt:      4.0 +/- 0.3  new
               2.7 +/- 0.3  used

Balance shaft
belt:          2.7 +/- 0.3  used or new

The higher value for the new cam belt is of course to compensate for
some stretching.  A new belt should be readjusted after 1000 miles to
the 2.7 value.  I have no idea why the same stretching considerations
do not seem to be applied to the balance shaft belt.  Since this belt
is not critical like the cam belt, we don't need to be as fussy about
it?

>I own a late model 1983 944 and recently shelled out to money for the
>camshaft and balance shaft belt tension tool (P9201).  My problem

I have successfully trained my mind so that whenever it thinks about
the "shelling" my wallet took to buy tool P9201, it immediately
recalls the hefty sums I used to pay mechanics who owned the tool
before I did, and I sleep well.

-Milo

P.S.: If you have replaced or removed (perhaps to install a new water
pump or reseal the engine ;-)) these belts, make sure you crank the
engine around (by hand) a couple of times to make sure you've gotten
the slack out before checking the tension.

Not on any VW or Porsche I've ever seen. 

	super-duper seals that do a lot better job than stock.  Lube the
	seals with silicone paste or a grease-type lubricant.  Dow-Corning
	111 compound works well here (Borrowing from Bruce Anderson's
	Performance Handbook).

I've never heard of that, but hey, if it works...
	
	Just turn the raised wheel to rotate the crank until
	the appropriate marking comes
	the sight hole as the markings are on the side
	toward the rear of the car.
	Not very convenient, unless some nice person
	painted the timing markings on
	the front of the impeller, hint, hint.

Doesn't the pulley on a 914 have the appropriate TDC and BDC markings?

	If the engine is in the car, I don't think
	you can adjust the front passenger side cylinder
	because a suspension mount
	is in the way - you'll have to wait until you
	drop the engine to do that one.
	Unfortunately that is also the same one
	that is under the oil cooler and
	is notorious in Bugs for overheating.

Ummmm.......The passenger side forward cylinder would be #2. You're 
thinking of #3, the driver's side forward cylinder. I don't know if 
there really is a suspension mount in the way for cylinders 2 or 3, 
but that would really be an insupportable situation! Not adjusting
one cylinder (especially #3) is a bad juju situation....

	   My description is a bit unorganized, but it should contain all the
	pertinent information - use the Heinous (Haynes) manual as a reference.

Or any manual. In fact, I always recommend the Idiot book by John Muir. It
gives a great discussion of doing the valves which should be applicable 
to your situation.
	
Oh yeah, Porsches (at least 356s and 912s) use 12mm nuts on the adjusters, 
not 13mm, as VWs do. Of course, what's on a stock 914 is a mystery to 
me. Just don't freak out if they're 12mm nuts....

Mount it where you can get at it. Forget the holes in your floor. 
If you're that worried about it, I'd think twice before driving
it at Road Atlanta. Seriously, I'm not trying to be an asshole
here, but I never put a car on the track that I'm not prepared 
to walk away from while the towing guys decide which junkyard
to take it to. 

I saw a guy stuff a brand-new Conquest into a wall at Laguna Seca 
this year. Your insurance won't pay you a dime if you stuff it 
at RA. The Conquest was totalled and this guy was bumming hard.  

And nothing lowers resale value like a fire in the car. And your
face is more important than resale value. So put the extinguisher
where it needs to be to save your life. A few holes through the
floor are no big deal....


EP Porsche Update:

I continued the teardown this weekend, though I have the Cold from 
Hell. I tried to stay in bed, really I did! I just couldn't help myself. 

I've made the decision to take the car apart to the last nut and bolt
and to put it back together with all new fasteners. In between, I'm 
going to have it dipped to get rid of all rust and glue and padding 
and paint and road crap. 

I haven't quite decided whether to keep the stock dash or try to carefully
cut it out of the car. It would be difficult to use the stock dash without
ruining it....I'm trying to decide if I can cut it out without ruining 
it, too. 

So the windows are out, the wiring harness all the way to the fusebox from
the front of the car is out, the interior is out, except for the underdash
wiring and gauges and switches. 

It's beginning to look a lot a skeleton.

The oil cooler is over the passenger side front cylinder - whatever
number it happens to be.  This is the same one blocked by the suspension
mount (my mount was redone in quarter inch plate steel, thanks to the
wonderful battery box - maybe it gives you less clearance than stock).
The suspension mount doesn't give you enough room to get a screwdriver
in there, but you can fit the wrench in there.  Maybe if you
had a trick screwdriver (like from a skateboard or something) it would
work, or you could just do trial and error until you got the right gap.
I just did it when I dropped the engine.

Oooo... I'm not so sure I'd place an "I want to buy a car" ad in Excellence,
or any nationally distributed mag, unless there was something *REALLY* 
specific that you were looking for, like say a '72 911S sunroof coupe with
the SportoMatic transmission (total of <50 made). (Incidentally, if you
know where one is, do call....)

First off, go get a copy of Bruce Anderson's book "The 911 Performance 
Handbook" from which you can learn all of the gotchas on the whole
family. Figure out what you can and can't live with, and for the stuff
you can't live with, figure out if you can replace it with aftermarket
stuff.

For example, living in the humidity capital of the world, also in the
middle of the SunBelt, it gets bloody hot here in the summer, and it's
always damp even in winter. So you really need to be able to run the
A/C compressor to de-humdify even your heat or defroster. Well, you don't
get that until you get into the '84 and up Carrera series. Not much
you can do about that. If on the other hand, you think the 20mm rotors
and wimpy brakes on the SC's suck, you *CAN* replace them with 24mm rotors
and Bremtek calipers, and even go to 935 style jumbo master cylinders if
you want to cut down the stroke (at the expense of raising effort).

Anyway, once you figure this stuff out, you need to budget some $$$ for
"upfixen der Porsche" and some more $$$ for "gogetten der Porsche en der
first place". You can trade them off against each other, but I generally
allow at least $1000 per every 4 years of age, just to be safe.

Then go shopping. Look at lots of cars. Talk your friends into looking at
cars for you. Go to PCA meetings and ask folks there. Check out the 
want ads in the back of Panorama (less of the gold-chain crowd trying to
make a buck off of the "exotic German investment" than in Excellence or
AutoWeak [yes, I spelled it that way intentially]).

And, above all, plan on treating your first 911 as a learning experience.
That means you acknowledge up front that you're not going to get your dream 
car, because you really don't know what that means yet, and that it's OK to 
plan on selling it in a year or so and getting another. Or, if you're
like me, not selling it in a year or so, and getting another anyway....
(I did debate over whether to call this "porschephiles" or "porscheholics"
with good reason.... "my name is Stan, and I've got this problem...")

Enjoy! Good luck!!! Good hunting!!! And let us all know what you get!

Stan

Subject: Re: Fire Extinguisher - where/how to mount???
Message-Id: <05901105213450/0003844665NB2EM@mcimail.com>

Well, ahem, there are two common ways of mounting extinguishers that I've seen
in 944 series cars.  First is bolted to a home-made steel bracket that bolts
under the front seat track (either side).  This is a clean, removable job but
requires that you get some steel and do a little cutting and bolting.  Not too
bad really.

Second, drill the chassis or tunnel in a place that doesn't interfere with seat
travel or hit anything on the back side.  Mine is in the rear, on the passenger
side vertical wall (behind a hypothetical passenger's heels), at floor level. 
This is very secure and I've had no problems in 4 years.  Only thing is that
when you drill, make sure you don't hit the fuel lines on the other side, in
front of the torsion spring cross-tube!

Drilling the car isn't such a big deal.  It drills like putty, and if you secure
the bolts with lock washers and spray rubberized undercoating all over, you'll
have no leak or rust problem.  Plus, on the interior you're drilling through
carpet, so the hole won't show when/if you remove the bolts.

JZ has an excellent point; the fire bottle is there to protect you - don't worry
an inordinate amount about how pretty it is.  Make sure you can get to it while
you're seated in the driver's seat!

[Editorial follows]

On a related note, I'd like to share an opinion with track folks and get input
from you all.  Several of us in New England have been talking about the
extinguisher rule lately, and have come to the conclusion it's pretty useless. 
I'm considering recommending that it not be required for Green (beginner), and
possibly more/all groups.  The reasoning is as follows:

1) Fires are extremely rare in our experience.
2) Fires are electrical or gas related.  You often can't get to the wires
causing an electrial fire, and fuel-fed fires can be so big that you just want
to protect your life (explosion risk, non-stop flames, etc).
3) Requiring a mounted bottle for beginners is a deterent to participation;
people don't want to spend $50-$100 just to *try* a track once.  They also don't
want to drill their car.
4) If there is a fire, a 2.5 pound bottle won't help very much.
5) Many other participants, and all flag stations, have extinguishers.
6) It doesn't make sense to require extinguishers but not require fireproof
clothing and helmets.

So basically, we feel that although it's better to have an extinguisher than
not, it provides little real protection and hinders new-comers.  If we're really
worried about fire, then let's require suits, and SA85 helmets.  M85 helmets
aren't required to be fireproof.  Zone I people don't even know that!  Also, the
biggest "safety" issue in most track incidents is extrication, not fire.  People
have to get out of the car quickly in case there *is* a fire hazard.  Yet we
don't talk about leaving doors unlocked, or having all windows down (to climb
through).

Comments and flames are encouraged!

                JBS

Cc: porschephiles <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Subject: Re: Fire Extinguisher - where/how to mount???
Message-Id: <40901106040504/0004056890NB3EM@mcimail.com>

I'll let you in on how I did mine.  It is by no means ideal (yet), but works and
avoids drilling holes in the floor and carpet.  Btw, if you do decide to go
through the floor, be real careful of you fuel lines!  Also make sure the
extinguisher you purchase has a metal exhaust tip, not plastic.  Imparts
generally has them for a very good price.

The rule is "metal-to-metal" hereabouts.  What I chose to do was mount a 2 1/2
lb. extinguisher in front of the passenger-side front seat.  Two L-shaped pieces
of flat stock are bolted, one to each top chair rail.  (The top rails are fixed
to the seat and travel with it.  They lock into and slide with the bottom rails,
which are bolted to the floor.)  The bottom of each 'L' bolts to the front of
the the corresponding rail, using a 1/4" (or M6) bolt, split washer, and nut.

A cross-piece of flat stock joins the tops of the two L's.  The extiniguisher is
bolted to this cross piece.  The whole shebang travels with the seat and fits
under any passenger's knees without getting in the way.  I positioned the nozzle
at the far end, facing down.  In the case of any accidental discharge, it is
more likely to go down into the floor rather than up onto the windscreen!

You will have to remove the seat to drill the holes in the rails.  It is held
down by six allen bolts.  When you put it back in, position the bolts very
carefully; it is easy to cross-thread them.  Drape the door sill while doing
this to keep from scratching it.

I enhanced this original set-up a bit to make it less wiggly.  The L's now have
back-to-back L's which brace them against the floor.  They just sit on the
floor; they're not drilled in.  I plan to redesign this over the winter,
hopefully using angle stock so to obviate the "need" for the second L's.  The
bracket has been primed then sprayed matte black.  It is easy to remove if
desired.

I do not like to see extinguishers mounted in the rear (cf. Timmins' & Selders'
postings).  If you believe an extinguisher belongs in a car, it should be easily
accessible under what is likely to be a panic situation.  If you're panicked, or
if you're on a side or a roof, navigating even a 2 1/2 lb. extinguisher out from
behind the seat (which may have moved during impact) is very hard.

Another thing 944 owners used to do was put down the rear seat and mount the
extinguisher to the rear seat "back."  This was very flimsy, and the seat does
not lock down.  No longer allowed.

While JZ's statement about being prepared to walk way from any track car may
work for him, it goes 100% against the way we in Zone 1 of the PCA run track
events!  (And I would assume the other Zones as well!)  We run high quality,
fun, but safe events.  I cannot afford to trash my car, emotionally or
financially.  We want to encourage our members to get out and experience their
cars "in ihren schoensten Form."

In Mass., we are indeed covered by our standard-issue insurance policies against
damage which occurs at the track so long as the event was a school and not a
competitive event.  So, except for the small number of time trials held, Mass.
drivers are in theory covered.  I say "in theory" because I'm not an attorney
nor am I an insurance agent.  I do know of several people who have made
forthright claims from incidents at the track, and no-one was denied
compensation, to my knowledge.  Of course, things are probably different in each
state.  Ask your broker/agent, and if the answer's 'yes,' get it in writing.

Finally, I agree with Jim Selder's comment regarding the need for extinguishers.
While I have nothing against them in cars, I think that requiring them for
"safety" makes very little sense.  There are much better things we can do which
bring bigger wins in safety, the least of which is properly training corner
workers.  Some Regions do this very well, most (in my experience) do not.  (By
way of comparison, the SCCA's workers are top notch.)

I would really enjoy hearing other people's opinions on the need for
extinguishers and on track safety.

Kevin Gross
Opining once again with the strength of ten normal curmudgeons.

>Did you see the snide, but accurate quote in Autofreak from somebody
> (I think in >CART) who said, at least Porsche can win in the Porsche
> Cup series...

Here's my editor's column from August this year (so they stole it from me):

********
Air Cooled Advertiser    August 1990

Nuts and Bolts                    Chris Welty

It all started, really, with racing.  Were it not for racing, there is
no question that Porsche would not be what it is today.  However,
currently Porsche is floundering in a putrid sea of indecision, as it
desperately searches for an arena in which it can score motor sport
victories.  Currently, the only series Porsche seems to have reliable
and consistent success in is the Porsche Cup.  As a result, they have
sent some of their top research engineers to study that series and
determine what the key difference is that makes Porsche a contender
there, while failing in so many other places.  After detailed and
lengthy analyses, they determined that in order to be assured a
victory in any class, they merely needed to eradicate the competition.

As many people know, before I was offered the job as Co-Editor of the
Air Cooled Advertiser, I was a well known figure in international arms
smuggling, as well as the leader of an elite mercenary squad upon
which the movie "Delta Force" was based.  To the layman, I was sort of
a "James Bond" for hire.  One day it hit me that the pen was mightier
than the sword, and I traded in my uzi and rocket launcher for a word
processor and Porsche (which, although perhaps not mightier than the
sword, is certainly more fun to drive).  However, my reputation had been so
deeply established in the underworld that few believed I had left for
good, and somehow the news of my retirement never spread.

It should have been no surprise, therefore, when the call came.  Never
the less, I was rather taken aback to hear Hans Branitzki, CEO of Porsche
AG, on the line.  He was quite insistent, and - after numerous refusals
on my part - he turned to blackmail, claiming to have in his possession 
some films of me and this Swedish number I knew in the old days.  When I
reminded him that those films had been a gift, he switched tactics
(remembering that you can attract more flies with honey), and mentioned
that the payment would be BB-PW 959, the prototype 959.  The first.

Luckily we were not on videophone, because he mistook the silence on
my part as hesitation (when in reality I was collecting my jaw from
the floor), and threw in my well known favorite: a year's supply of
canned squid.  That was lucky since I had almost ran out from my last
job.  At this point I could do little else but agree.  It wasn't until
a few days later that I realized even with my connections in
government it would be impossible to bring a 959 into the US, so I
sent Hans my regrets.  I didn't really want it anyway.

This issue, which is late again, proves that we the editors can do
little to get a newsletter out on time if we have to write everything.
This month's tech article, aside from being humorous, is meant to
remind people that you shouldn't work on your car unless you know what
you're doing.





=====

Christopher Welty  ---  Asst. Director, RPI CS Labs | "Porsche:  Fahren in
weltyc@cs.rpi.edu             ...!njin!nyser!weltyc |  seiner schoensten Form"

	While JZ's statement about being prepared to walk
	way from any track car may
	work for him, it goes 100% against the way we in
	Zone 1 of the PCA run track
	events!  (And I would assume the other Zones as well!) 
	We run high quality,

Well, I suppose I should explain myself a little better. I don't go out
there thinking my car is a throwaway or that if it gets munched, it's 
no big deal. I guess the central thing, though, is that regardless of
how careful you are, the potential for a munch is always there. Therefore,
I wouldn't drive your 944 on the track, because it's not mine and my
insurance does not cover me driving your car under those conditions. 

Running your events with an eye towards keeping the cars in one piece
is good, but everyone should be aware that these things happen. I think 
it's a little naive to downplay it. 

I think everyone tries to run safe events, and while I try to maintain
a reasonable balance between driving fast and keeping my car in one piece, 
I can't vouch for the crazy &^$&^%@*)#&^$&^@$ millionaire who wants to 
pass me in the turns. 

I was specifically thinking of the fellow who was worried about his
resale value. I'd advise him to think long and hard about how he
wants to do this. It's a risk, but then again, probably less of a 
risk than my drive to work each morning....(mountain commute, y'know.) 

JZ

From: zursch%large.wpd.sgi.com@sgi.com (Kid Redshift)
Message-Id: <9011061913.AA24919@large.wpd.sgi.com>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Fire Extinguishers

	Several of us in New England have been talking about the
	extinguisher rule lately, and have come to the conclusion
	it's pretty useless. 
	I'm considering recommending that it not
	be required for Green (beginner), and
	possibly more/all groups. 

Well, I kind of like them, and I keep them in all my cars, but your mileage
may vary....I've also never had to use one in an emergency situation. I 
have come upon a burning car, too.....the guy was already out, and I 
took one look at that Honda Accord and at my little fire bottle and decided
it was a waste to even try. However, had the guy been pinned, the 
fire extinguisher would have been most handy in aiding his extraction. 

	The reasoning is as follows:
	1) Fires are extremely rare in our experience.

Yup. But boy oh boy, when they happen! 

	2) Fires are electrical or gas related. 
	You often can't get to the wires
	causing an electrial fire, and fuel-fed fires
	can be so big that you just want
	to protect your life (explosion risk, non-stop flames, etc).

Which is where the inside bottle comes into play. To give you the five 
or ten seconds to get yourself out of the car before it does serious 
injury to you.

	3) Requiring a mounted bottle for beginners
	is a deterent to participation;
	people don't want to spend $50-$100 just to
	*try* a track once.  They also don't
	want to drill their car.

Ummmmm.....are your track schools otherwise free? 
I'd say $20 is more like the cost of a decent 
car-sized fire bottle. Your mileage may vary.

	4) If there is a fire, a 2.5 pound bottle won't help very much.

But maybe just enough. 

	5) Many other participants, and all flag stations, have extinguishers.

Yup. Good thing, too.

	6) It doesn't make sense to require extinguishers
	but not require fireproof clothing and helmets.
	
That's true. A Helmet requirement seems reasonable. But lacking that,
and if you will believe me that an adequate fire bottle can be purchased for
$20 and might possibly save your face in a marginal situation, I don't think
many people would be that cheap....though I've seen examples....

	So basically, we feel that although it's
	better to have an extinguisher than
	not, it provides little real protection and hinders new-comers. 

I think it is better to have one, and the little protection it gives 
comes pretty cheap. 

	If we're really worried about fire, then let's require suits,
	and SA85 helmets.  M85 helmets aren't required to be fireproof. 
	Zone I people don't even know that! 

I didn't know that, either. Makes me glad I have an SA85....But about your
first point, there's a clear cost/benefit ratio here. Requiring a fire
suit would indeed scare away people. Even requiring *any* sort of helmet
would tend to thin the ranks. But contrariwise, you enhance the attitude
of the drivers by requiring some safety gear. I mean, if I'm wearing a 
helmet, it ain't toodling to work..... I think the cost/benefit of 
a fire bottle is worth it. As you point out, fires are rare, so the 
person makes a one time investment and then has the bottle for next
time! (or for extracting his/her spouse/kid/friend/etc. on the street....)

	Also, the biggest "safety" issue in most track incidents is
	extrication, not fire.  People
	have to get out of the car quickly in case there *is*
	a fire hazard.

As I pointed out, the fire bottle is there to give
you an extra 10 seconds in the event of a fire. 
	
	Yet we don't talk about leaving doors unlocked, or having
	all windows down (to climb through).

You should. Driver safety is something that every school or open track
I've ever seen has spent a lot of time with. You want to encourage the
right mindset, otherwise you invite trouble in a couple of ways. First,
they won't know what to do if they're in trouble. Second, they won't 
take the thing seriously enough so they'll be more likely to get in trouble
in the first place! The guy who stuffed his Conquest at Laguna Seca 
didn't show up to the safety meeting and sat in the back of the room 
wearing shades and looking bored when we discussed how to spin off the
track gracefully. The instructor asked him specifically if he would
like to join the group and he said "I know all this stuff." (yes, a 
direct quote.) He then did *exactly* the wrong thing and ruined his car 
because of his bad attitude. 

	Comments and flames are encouraged!
	
No flames, this is intended to be persuasion by reason, not argument.
It's a tradeoff. When I used to ride a motorcycle,
I always wore a helmet. Now, a helmet on a bike
won't save your life in a clear cut horrible accident, like getting 
run over by a UPS van. But it can save your life in some situations and
more importantly, it will keep you from being maimed in a non-life
threatening situation.....likewise a fire bottle can help prevent you
from getting 2nd degree burns on your hands and face as you climb out....

Yours for quality track time, 

JZ

Subject: Re: Fire Extinguishers
Message-Id: <83901106232938/0003844665NB3EM@mcimail.com>

Your point of the extinguisher being used as a time-extender is a good one.  It
can be nice to have an extra 10 seconds to get out of a burning car (except, see
below).

A Halon 2.5 lb bottle with metal discharge nozzle and bracket is about $40-$50
mail order, and more in stores, plus installation if the driver can't do it
him/her self.  It's not too bad for most people, but it's just another expense
on top of a helmet, etc for a beginner.  I certainly wouldn't trade my potetial
safety to save so little money.

There's an implication in your discussion that needs further examination.  The
idea of the extinguisher giving you a few extra seconds is nice, but not as
valid as it first appears.  That's because it takes you several seconds, maybe
5-10, just to get the thing out of its bracket, pull the pin, aim and shoot. 
And remember that you've (possibly) just had an accident or are slowing down
from a high speed, so you don't have full concentration.  I'd rather spend my
time getting out of the car than fiddling for the fire bottle.

I agree with you completely that everyone *should* have one, but is it worth
*requiring* it, considering all the afore-mentioned arguments?  The little
squirt of halon and the difficult getting it isn't like a permanent,
"professional" system where you just hit a button and the thing shoots through
plumbing all over the car.  But I think that some people treat the extinguisher
as if it were that easy to use.

Anyway, these are all just open thoughts on the subject.  The feedback is useful
and helps illustrate other ideas and opinions.  Thanks for your input!

                JBS

I just sold an 1985 1/2 (the new dash and insides) in Dallas for $13K.
>From the sound of it, my car was in much worse shape.  I had about 67K
miles and a lot of cosmetic flaws (cracked dash, door pings, etc).

Subject: File Extinguishers

Not knowing anything about the topic, I was a little surprised that
the file extinguishers you guys are talking about require the driver
to get it out of its bracket, pull a pin, aim, shoot, etc.

If Halon gas is all that it is cracked up to be, then I would assume
that one, two, or at most three jets in the right places would kill
any fire.  Especially since the car is reasonably air tight except at
the windows (i.e. its not like your driving a dune buggy or
something).

I think I would be tempted to mount it on the side passenger's seat
back with the nozzle pointed down towards the driver's lap.  The idea
being that you are trying to flood the floor with Halon gas.  If you
can split the spray and hit both floors, all the better.  Rig it so
that it will discharge when a pin is pulled.  So when you hit the
wall, you pull the pin and head for the door.  As a final trick, add a
fire detector to discharge it in the case where the driver is
unconscience.

Also, I would assume (although I've never seen it) that if the gas
tank blows, you're in a heap of hurt and a little bottle of Halon gas
won't do much good anyhow.

Am I totally out to lunch? -- hot dogs anyone?

pedz

From: sai@corp.sun.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs [Wordsmith])
Message-Id: <9011070209.AA12784@formula1.Corp.Sun.COM>
To: merithew@jessica.stanford.edu
Subject: Re:  Rusty Exhaust: how to treat?
Cc: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu

 If it's rusty it's going to give out sooner or
 later. Don't bother wire brushing it, you'll just
 thin the metal and give the rust nice new metal
 to oxidize. Use a rusty metal primer (from Rustoleum
 paint, available at Scherba's on El Camino in Menlo Park
 or a cold steel galvanizing spray). Do this after
 hot tanking the parts in solvent (to remove all the
 built-up oil).

 Forget it. Just scrap it and order some stainless
 steel heat exchangers from Mid-Engine Mania in
 San Luis Obispo. 805-549-8525. They can get them to
 you for $185 each (sale price). Really good buy.
 Internal diameter is slightly larger than stock
 so you get a mild performance increase.

 Then put on a Bursch exhaust and you'll be one happy
 puppy. Forget Anza and Monza crap. Gone through at
 least three of those exhaust systems (they rust out
 QUICK). I've seen some figures which say they actually hurt
 your horsepower.

 Best of luck.
 Stefan

I saw a '91 944S2 on a dealer's lot this AM.  The changes are minor:

  1. The rear air dam as it appeared on the '90 Euro turbo.  Solid loop above
     the window.

  2. Redesigned rear wiper (huh?)

  3. Different radio (Zzzz....)

The front PU valence is unchanged, and is not as expected from the spy photos
(and the Ricoh give-away ad: see pg. 83 in the 29 Oct Forbes).

Production of the current 944 series is to cease permanently in December of this
year, we are told.  They will be replaced by a car with a new chassis, and a
six-cylinder engine (and that likely a V, since the boxer won't fit).  Porsche
will be building them 100% themselves, like the 911- and 928-series cars.

Out of the mouths of salesmen...

To: "Steve.Fleagle" <Steve.Fleagle@mel.cipl.uiowa.edu>
To: porschephiles <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Cc: school <school@sol.crd.ge.com>
Subject: Re: mounting a harness
Message-Id: <20901107034602/0004056890NB1EM@mcimail.com>

The only quality harness installation I've seen in a 944-series car is Elizabeth
Ames', done for her by RST in Bow, NH.  They took the two shoulder belts back
through the horizontal split in the rear fold-down seat, and mounted them into
the rear deck.  Since the holes are under the removeable rear carpetting, no
damage, visible or otherwise, was done to the car.  The angle was right...

And speaking of angle, I believe the rule is that the shoulder belts cannot
incline at more than a 45 degree angle.  (I forget whose rule this is, SCCA or
PCA, maybe both.)  This rules out use of the bolt holes for the two-point rear
seat belts.  One idea I have not seen tried is a fixed bar between the two top
mounting bolts for rear three-point harnesses.  The harness would be attached to
this bar, much as it is attached to a real roll cage.  Anyone tried this?

Five- and six-point harnesses are wonderful.  But a good first step would be a
simple lap belt.

One other thing to try is a locking plate with the car's three-point belt.  This
is a simple piece of metal which cinches the inertial belt for you, sort of like
a D-ring belt buckle does.  Datsun (oops, Nissan) sells them for a few bucks a
piece, and they come with most child seats.  Instant three-point harness.

Kevin Gross

Look in your Yellow Pages (no, not on your workstation 8{) ) under
"Metalizing" or "Metal Treatments" or similar. What you're looking
for is a shop that can aluminize your exhaust. Should cost you 
about $150 to have the heat exchangers and muffler done. The process
involves sand blasting followed by spraying hot aluminum emulsion 
onto the metal. If you look at ads for "lifetime mufflers", you find
that's how most of them are treated...

Stan

Perry, I think you're talking about permanently installed halon system in closed
race cars.  What you described is almost exactly what is done, in race cars.

In our street cars, we have little hand-held extinguishers that must be bolted
to the chassis (or rollcage, for the few who have them).  Our problem is very
different.

anything besides metal.  The rear seatback, besides not being locked down, is
made of wood in 944s.  This won't do.  It's gotta be either the seat rails or
the chassis itself.

Alex, don't despair.  There are MANY nice cars all over the U.S.  Although the
concentration of them is higher in SoCal, they exist everywhere.  A good number
of people store their cars during the winter, so you can get a clean, rust-free
car even in New Hampshire.  In fact, it may have lower miles and less ozone &
sunlight degradation than a California car.  LA's air isn't exactly clean...

Late model 911s have zinc treated bodies, so you won't find much rust on them. 
It's really the older 911s and 914s that have problems.  Each car has specific
areas to look at, such as battery trays, rocker panels, A-pillars, sway bar
mounts, etc.  There will be a couple books on the subject, I'm sure.  It's best
to peruse the books in a store to filter the less useful ones out.

Also, use old fashioned common sense.  If you get serious about buying a car,
send the owner a few bucks and get him/her to send good color photos, with
close-ups in the trouble areas of the body.  Ask about maintenance and crash
history, and if the car's seen much track use.

Judge the overall appearance of the car cosmetically and mechanically.  If it
looks like the one you want, fly out and buy it!  It IS hard to buy a car long
distance, but with enough time and questions and phone calls you can do it.  I'd
suggest looking for the best car you can possibly afford, though - it isn't
worth all this effort and travel/expense to get an "OK" car.

One rule of thumb I've heard others mention and I've seen myself: the vehicle's
overall care reflects the owner's mindset.  If the owner is neat, organized, and
meticulous, then the car is likely to be in better shape.  If the owner is a
space shot, or appears to be more relaxed in maintaining his/her possesions,
then the car will probably suffer the same thing.  People are consistent in
behavior for the most part.  A clean, well maintained car is a better gamble
than one that's obviously a little worn.

Good luck on your search.  I hope you find something that is fun for you!

                JBS


P.S. - yes, Hemmings is an industry standard.  It's an excellent source for very
interesting cars!  Also try Autoweek and Road & Track for general interest, and
Panorama (Porsche Club of America) for lots of used Porsches all over the U.S.

To: mking@aerospace.aero.org, porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Re:  Heater Problem (914)

The heaters on 914's, when working correctly, can melt most commonly
used metals 8{)  OK, so that's not quite it, but you get the idea.
If it's not working, you have (chose at least one)

* missing parts in the heated air path (flapper boxes, heat exchangers,
  the various bits of plumbing that connect it all up, hoses, etc)

* broken parts in the controls (cables, flaps, etc)

* the coolest running air-cooled engine I've ever heard about.

Based on experiences with my 914, I'd guess that you have missing
parts. Here's the drill: if you get oil leaks (and what 914 doesn't have
oil leaks?) you end up inadvertently lubricating the screws that hold some
of the sheet metal together, and when it vibrates (and what 914 doesn't?)
they, well, fall out. Causing the whole thing to be a bit more ventilated
than it might otherwise be. 

Suggestion: get a copy of the microfiche sheet that shows the parts of the
heater plumbing (you dealer can turn fiche into paper - usually) and 
get under the car and see what you're missing. It is less messy but more
expensive, but you could also take it to the shop and let them do it....

Stan

Subject: Re: Heater Problem (914)


Well, first of all, welcome to the wonderful world of 914's :-). The
first thing to do is to get down on your hands and knees and ... no,
not pray, but check under the car to see whether you have a heat
exchanger. If not, well    GET ONE! :-). Actually, that should be the
SECOND thing to do. The first one being to get a Hayne's manual for
the car, 'cos you're going to need it. Nothing against 914
mechanicals, mind you, but it's a lot more fun and cheaper to do
yourself all those little adjustments that are required.

If you DO have heat exchangers, the next thing to check is whether
you're actually using them (no offense intended, but when my dad drove
my 914 for the first time, he didn't even know it had heater controls
on the dash - not that they're hard to miss).

The next weak link in the chain is the blower motors that get that
wonderfully aromatic hot (well, warm) air into the cockpit. As I said,
the best thing to do is to get a manual and go through the debugging
routine. It's very straightforward. And don't believe people who tell
you that you can't get warm in a 914. I have driven mine in Montreal
winters without any problems. Good luck.

toufic

Well, last year my 914 was less expensive than my wife's Beetle
to insure. We go for the "whole enchilada" -- liability, comp/collision,
glass, etc. so it's still pretty serious money for cars that age. We
also (based on a *NASTY* experience I had with State Farm some years ago)
only have "stated value" policies written for those cars. Sure, I have
to look harder to find an agent who can do it, and I wind up with some
strange companies (like Crum & Forester [a.k.a. Xerox Financial Services])
but it's worth it to *KNOW* that the car is really covered if anything 
happens.

For the 911, I just get full coverage on a "straight" policy. Ditto for 
the Hyundai Sonata. Intersting to note that the 911 insurance is only 
about $350/yr. more than the Sonata.....

For the next year, the renewal notice sez that my 914 and 911 will jump by 
about 50%, while the Beetle and Sonata stay the same. Looks like my insurance
company is trying to Darwinize me off of their rolls...

Stan

Alex,
	Believe it or not, I understand your quandry.  I went out at
age 25 (minus two months) and bought a 1980 928.  I had looked at
several sports cars and the "male driver under 25" clause really sucks.
I stayed with USAA where I had to pay $900 for the first six month
policy (some of that I got back once the bean counters figured out
I had turned 25) -- and that was with no points in two years, and
never an accident... Oops, better knock on wood for saying that...
Anyway, the moral of the story is you're gonna have to pay quite
a lot in insurance until you turn 25.  Sorry, it's unfair, but that's
the way it is.  The good news (if there is any) is that right now I
pay less than $700 a year (in South Carolina).  That will go to about
$1000 when I actually move to the Bay Area.
	To answer your question, I suggest you look at State Farm
or maybe Nationwide.  Allstate has always been too high from the
experiences of my friends (I have always been with USAA but they only
insure military officers, former officers, dependants, and former
dependants -- I now fill the last category :-) and the most obnoxious
part of it is that Allstate tends to drop people after an accident.  I
have to say that I'd refuse to talk to Allstate until they change their
attitude.  Try State Farm first: I have a 24 year old friend with a
1988 951 (944 turbo) that pays less than $1400 a year, with a speeding
ticket (55/30!).
	Good luck and enjoy your Porsche!

		--> bert

Subject: Re: 944 Belt Tensioning Values

>That's great!  Do you notice any variance across measurements?

Sorry, but I have been out of town (and therefore off the air)
most of this week.

I am sure my many measurements were unnecessary.  The main differences
occurred (as you might guess) before and after I cranked the engine
around by hand.  Although I tried to pretension the belt as best I
could when wrapping the belt around its various pulleys, it still took
a couple of turns of the crank to evenly distribute any slack around
the belt.

The other important trick (as you probably already know) is that after
you have cranked the engine around and back to TDC you have to turn
the crank backwards about 10 degrees (which corresponds to about one
and a half teeth on the cam pulley) in order to remove any uneven
tension induced by the cranking.  It was this part of the procedure
that my neurosis had me double and triple checking.  I once saw a
professional Porsche mechanic perform this operation.  He didn't try
to measure crank angle or count cam pulley teeth -- he just gave the
breaker bar he had attached to the crank nut a quick firm jerk to
unload the belt pretension.  My current procedure is therefore a
combination of "cranking" and "jerking/teeth counting" until I am
satisfied that there is no slack hiding out between a pair of pulleys
somewhere.

By the way, regarding the correct tensioning values Jim Selders
recently posted:

>The lower settings are the correct ones.  Porsche reduced the values when they
>came out with the improved belts a few years ago.  The new belts don't require
>the tension of the older ones.

Jim, to make sure we are all on the same page, could you please give
the numerical values of the "lower settings".  I would also be curious
to know which manual supplement or tech bulletin is the most recent
authoritative pronouncement on this topic.

-Milo

Subject: Harness in 951.

I have had a bunch of positive feedback on my setupo, so here goes:

Sub belt:  remove seat and place just behind crossmember along front of
seat

Lf waist belt: remove seat belt attatchement and replace bolt with
harness eyebolt.

Rt waist bolt: drill a hole in the trans tunnel.  When bolt io removed,
carpet will obscure 3/8" (approx) hole.  Screw in eyebolt and make sue
to use the supplied big washers on underside of car;

Rear mounts:  Now THIS is the trick setup:

Remove rear carpet.
Look at the area by the drivers turn signal.
On angled sestion, 2 bolts can be placed.  These must be drilled fron
underneath.  Beleive it or not this can be done without removing the
muffler.  You may wish to drop it a few inches but it really isn't
necessary.  Again, use big washers but on BOTH sides of he sheetmetal.

This setup is the only one Ive seen which is safe (low angle of
seatbelt: no compression, can be run with the seat up or down, and which
hides the rear mounts when not in use.  You will need to use the long
sholder harnesses.  Also, you will need to remove rear carpet at the
track.

The following reprinted without permission from the "Windblown
Witness", San Diego Region PCA Newsletter, November 1990

...some of the juicy gossip out of Porsche AG... It seems that the
product development for the mid 1990's includes two instead of three
product lines and development of a full four seat and possibly "four
door" sports car to replace the 928.  This car could have a short
snout, large cabin and sports car shape.  The new, premium-priced four
seater is not destined to arrive before 1995.  That means a full 18
years since the intro of the 928 here in San Diego in 1977.  They say
the 911 (now known internally as the 964) will be around to the year
2000.  Sou you can figure that the 928 and 944 will drop out of the
line up around 1995.  The 944 will be freshened up for 1991 and will
feature a new front and rear end and some interior improvements and
more output from the 3.0 liter, 4-cylinder engine.  There is even a
rumor that the air-cooled six in the rear of the 911 could be replaced
by a narrow-angle V-8 that is being developed for the 928 and would
displace 3.5 to 4 liters, receive four-valve technology and develop up
to 400 horsepower...

Tom Hauser Jr.
President

Milo, I think the tension values you and Kevin emailed are the correct ones. 
They used to be at 4.0, or somewhere nearby, for new AND used belts.  Apparently
they reduced the second number to 2.7, so the belt can relax and have a long,
happy life after an initial stressing.

It's important to have a good, new version belt that's tensioned correctly.  But
it doesn't matter so much if it's 2.6 or 2.8, or maybe with some imperfect bit
of slack.  As long as the belt won't jump teeth, and isn't excessively tight,
then you'll be safe.  Of course, the belt also shouldn't resonate and hit the
belt cover, or be unstable at sustained high rpm.  But as long as it's good and
snug and stays out of trouble, it'll work.

What I'm trying to do is sooth some of the excitedness (?sp) that often
accompanies 944 belt tensioning.  It's only a problem if ignored or very badly
done.

                JBS

The '89 951s are basically the 944 Turbo S model of '88.  I believe there are no
differences other than the dropping of 'S'.  It has the 'S' chassis and 247 hp
engine.  And yes, it has all the "features" of the #2 bearing...  If you run
Mobil 1 15W-50, change it often, and replace the bottom-end bearings every 40
track days (60 hours or so), you should have no problem.  "Your mileage may
vary"...

They're not greatly changed from earlier years.  The horsepower was increased to
about 247 hp by, one would assume, making some of the same changes that the
AutoThority people did to the chips.  All sorts of fairly minor engine
improvements have been made over the years.  The '89 Turbos got improved exhaust
valves and stem seals, for example.

Outside of the engine, it got hardened 1st and 2nd gears in the transmission,
and a slightly different limited slip diff (I forget the changes).  The big news
was the 928S4-heritage front spindles, rotors, and brakes.  In fact, some of the
parts are 928 parts.  The rear fenders got their lips folded to accomondate 9"
rims.

Kevin Gross

I contacted two different brokers.  The one I settled on was Bradford
Auto Consultants, (408) 394-7704.  Their charge is incredibly
reasonable: $200, payable if they find a car, and includes road testing
and mechanical inspection.  They specialize in finding used, classic,
and muscle cars.  While they are not Porsche specialists, they have a
lot of practical experience, and know the questions to ask.  They will
travel to see non-local cars for you, but of course that adds to the
cost.  The broker that I dealt with is Jerry Bradford.  His dad, Ace,
raced VW powered dune-buggies back in the 60's, 70's, and 80's (perhaps
he still does - the shop always has a couple in various stages of repair).
Prior to opening his own shop, he ran the used car repair dept. for
Wester VW Porsche Audi.  So, there is a lot of VW-Porsche experience
in the family.

BTW, I phoned Jerry on a Thursday.  By that evening, he had located
several cars that seemed to meet my specs, and had phoned the owner of
the car in Fresno that I ended up purchasing.  I took Friday off, drove
to Fresno, and bought the car.  Jerry was trusting enough to set up the
appointment for me without meeting me, without any contract between us.
I paid him the next week.  It is nice to do business with people like
that.

The second person that I contacted was Dave Terdy, who is the president
of the local PCA chapter, and who owns a business which specializes in
Porsche buying services.  He has a list of cars for sale, which is
available in either single copy or subscription service, and he has
developed a series of videotapes which offer pointers on what to look
for when buying a used Porsche.  His company is called Driven by Design,
and is located in Carmel (408-625-1393).  If Jerry Bradford had not
been able to deliver, I definitely would have gone this route.

-Dave Erickson

Subject: 914 autox preparation

Note: this is going to the autox, porschephiles, and info-vw mailing lists.
Please direct your replies appropriately, to some combination of me, autox,
porschephiles, and info-vw.  Is 914 stuff inappropriate for info-vw?  Do all
the relevant people also hang out on porschephiles?

I just purchased a '73 914 2.0, which I plan to build into an autocrosser.
I have a number of particular questions, and then am looking for some
free-form help.

First of all, the car nominally has California emissions.  A 914 autox
specialist tells me that there's no difference between the CA cars and the
49-states cars, other than a sticker on the windshield saying that the car
passes CA emissions.  Can anyone else back that up?

The car is in excellent condition; no significant rust anywhere (the only
rust is on the surface, and it's at the back of the floor of the rear
trunk).  It is mechanically very strong, and compression reads 135 for #1,
#2, and #3, and 125 for #4.  It does seem to have a significant oil leak
somewhere, not sure where yet.  (I did just get it this past weekend.)  It
has approximately 103K miles on it, and basically nothing has been done to
it.  I have (almost) all the service records from the time the car was new -
the only significant work was new clutch and related parts and repacked CV
joints at about 70K miles.  Other than that, it's completely original, down
to the 165R15 tires (suggesting that it's never even been driven hard).

Now, I'm planning on keeping the car in Stock category for the '91 season,
and then moving to Street Prepared in '92.  I'm not wealthy, and would
rather not do anything twice.  It seems to me that the smart move is to do
everything that's legal for both categories, and leave everything else
alone.  The particular help I need from Team.Net (the autox people) is
creating this list, and then I need help determining if the stuff that's
missing is important.  Note: for those who aren't aware, in Stock most
things must be off of a '73 914 2.0.  In Street Prepared, I can trade just
about anything between any 914 2.0 and any 914-6, regardless of year.

For instance, I will probably want to replace all the suspension bushings,
since they're 100K miles old.  But in Stock, they have to be stock rubber
bushings.  In Street Prepared, I can use harder rubber or something like
that.  Do I want to do this twice?  What other things have similar issues?

Here's what I've come up with so far:

Engine: Fortunately, I believe the engine internals will be the same for
	both categories, and only the intake, exhaust, and distributor will
	change.  So I should build the engine this winter, because it's the
	same for both?  True?

Gearbox: Leave it untouched for Stock.  Next year in SP, I know that the
	914-6 gearbox had different ratios.  Are they better?  Also, aren't
	all the 914-6 gearboxes the rear-linkage models, while the 2.0's
	have the (better?) side-linkage?  In SP the linkage can be
	modified.  Is there anything that can be done to make it less vague
	with either box?  I would definitely shorten the throws.  I'd also
	try to find a good limited slip in SP, preferably a Torsen.  Are
	these available for this gearbox?

Suspension: In Stock, all I'll do is put in the Koni's and play with sway
	bars.  The car has the factory-optional sway bars; do I want both of
	them in Stock?  I can use any front bar, and can use either no rear
	bar or the factory-optional one.  Suggestions?  In SP I'll want
	lower rear springs, stiffer front torsion bars, and harder bushings
	(including steering bushings).  Struts and sway bars will remain the
	same.  How about stress bars?  What should I do about the suspension
	bushing question?  Replace them now and then replace them again
	later?  Is this a big deal?

Interior: Probably won't touch it for Stock, other than maybe changing the
	steering wheel.  Are there any fully padded and upholstered racing
	seats that will fit for SP?  It's awfully tight...  Any other common
	interior mods?  I'll put 5-point harnesses in both seats.  No roll
	bar or roll cage, if I can help it.

Wheels: It currently has aftermarket 15x5.5 wheels (the stock size), which
	I'll probably keep for Stock with 205/50-15 tires.  In SP, I assume
	I'll want the GT/916 fender flares, and maybe 225/50-15 on 8.5" rims
	up front and 245/45-16 on 9.5" rims in back.  True?  Or should I
	stick with the stock fenders and go with narrower tires to keep the
	car narrow?

What am I missing?  Do any of you SP people have complete lists of
everything you've done to the car?

Any and all help is appreciated.  I'd like to keep a list of people who are
good contacts for these sorts of questions...  If you're available for help
please let me know.  Thanks!

Yes Steve, your friend should come back after about 1000-2000 miles to get the
belt retensioned.  The tension must be reduced after an intial stretching /
break-in period.  He should NOT come back in "a few hundred miles"; this is too
soon.  I believe the number that Kevin Gross and Milo Dorr quoted was 2000
miles.

Tensioning the belt costs about 2 1/2 hours of labor at our local dealer; belt
replacement costs another $200-$250 if I recall correctly.  I know, $400 sounds
a little high for two new drive belts.  You're right, it is obscene!  I just 
bought a cam belt for a Rabbit/Golf/Audi for $6, and it'll last >75K miles!

Kevin points out the difference between the '86-'88 944 Turbo and the '88 944
Turbo S / '90 Turbo.  Note that the '89 "944 Turbo" *is* a "Turbo S"-type car. 
The lower-power engine, original brakes, and other pieces were no longer offered
in 1989.

The old 944 Turbo chassis (brakes, mostly) now appears as a 944 S2.

I heard news of a new Porsche model from a [usually well informed] sales rep at
a car show here last week.  Sounds like Porsche has secret products in the
pipeline to replace the 944 VERY soon.  He thinks it'll have a 4 cylinder
engine, but I've long heard rumors of a 6 going in.

Also, John Andretti's T-car was at the car show.  It is returning to Europe to
an unknown life.  Maybe some bar in Stuttgart will put it on the wall, and serve
Foster's beer from the fuel cell!

I'll summarize the maintenance costs for the models you quote:

1) 944's aren't too bad, as Porsches go.  That means several hundred bucks a
year if nothing much goes wrong.  Tires and shocks are expensive, but tune-up
parts are cheap.  The cam belt should be tensioned and examined for damage every
15K miles for maximum safety.  That will cost perhaps $100 in labor.

2) The 944S should be the same as the 944.  It's got mostly the same parts
except for the head.  The chain drive to the second cam is very reliable and
works wonders on the 4-valve 928s.  Again, keep after the cam belt.

3) The 944 Turbo is less reliable.  A number of them have turbo failures due to
oil leaking internally.  The drivetrain takes more power, so CV joints and
clutches wear faster.  The clutch guide sleeve fails routinely in 30-50K miles,
requiring about $1500-$2000 in labor to get to the clutch area.  The early
exhaust manifolds crack (these have mostly been replaced by Porsche with a
bellows-style expansion joint).  There have also been 3 fuel-line related
recalls on the car.  Some fuel lines leak/burst, causing a fire.

If you track any of the above cars, you can easily triple (or more!) your
maintenance cost, in addition to needing more brake pads and tires.  The 944
Turbos warp front brake rotors on tracks, unless you switch pad compounds.  944
Turbo brakes are the weakest point on the car.  Later models ('88 Turbo S, '89
Turbo) had 928 front brakes and these are much better.

BTW, if you get a model with air bags and they ever deploy, the repair cost is
very high.  The passenger side bag breaks the windshield, at about $1000.  The
bags themselves cost about $2000 or $2500 according to a very recent Autoweek
article.  So a minor bump that pops the bags could set you (or your insurance
company) back $3500, plus the scrape on your paint ($100).  Aren't air bags just
wonderful?

Opinions flowing freely,

                JBS

I usually just take mine down to the coin-op car wash, select the
"engine cleaner" option, hose it all down, then after letting it sit
for a few minutes, hose it down with the high-pressure gun.

if you do this for the top of the engine. I've done that, but be prepared
to do the usual stuff for reviving a wet engine....

Ummmm. I also remembered something else: the smell may well be coming
from your heat exchangers, which do a spectacular job of retaining oil
spilled from the usual pushrod/valve cover/oil pressure sender/oil cooler
leaks. You may need to pull the heat exchangers, and clean them at 
the same time...

	I just finished (I hope) fixing some oil leaks on my 914 (pushrod
	tube seals, valve cover gaskets) and I think I should have the
	engine steam cleaned so it stops smelling like burnt oil & grease
	and so I can locate other oil leaks more easily.

How did the valve adjust go? Steam cleaning is a very good idea at this
point, but you might find that you can get most of the cleaning done 
with foamy engine bright and Brake cleaning solvent for the really nasty
grime. 
	I have never had an engine steam cleaned - is there anything I should
	know or do first?  Will it ruin the valve cover gaskets I just
	finished putting in?  Other gaskets/hoses?  If I have to remove
	fuel injection parts, then it might not be worth it...

It won't require removing anything, and it won't ruin your valve cover
gaskets, either. But try a can or two of Foamy engine bright, or gunk,
or any other thing of a similar nature.....it might work well enough to
suit your purpose....

That's funny, the brand of oil shouldn't affect consumption much at all.  Use a
10W-40 for "normal" driving and a -50 for competition.

A quart every 600 miles is NOT normal, unless you're at the race track.  You may
have ring/valve guide problems, or the oil might be mixing with your water via a
bad water/oil intercooler or a blown head gasket.  Also, there could simply be a
leak, like at the back of the cam cover.

Simple Green also works wonders at cleaning oil, grease, and grit.

I did get an engine steam cleaned once, and I won't do it again.  It's so
effective that it removes undercoating, and attacks card/fiber- board.  If the
steam is left pointing at the edge of a gasket, I'm sure it will break it down
in a few seconds, due to extreme heat, water, and pressure.

I'd stick with what the others have mentioned - a good engine cleaner and a few
minutes of elbow grease.

                JBS

"Hemmings" is Hemmings Motor News, a thick, comprehensive list of all sorts of
specialty vehicles, new and old, from all over the world.  It's an excellent
source if you're trying to locate a specific, unusual car.

From: "David K. Boyd" <N025BQ@tamvm1.tamu.edu>
Subject:      Hemmings Motor News

In addition to being an excellent source for buying cars themselves,
Hemmings has all manner of listings for parts and services for more
interesting vehicles.

For example, I have an 1959 Mercedes-Benz 220S sedan.  The car has a
Blaupunkt radio with tubes and seek tuning that no longer works, and
in Hemmings I found a man who restores such ancient equipment.  It's
an excellent source for hard-to-find parts, and for people who are
parting out cars.

The only drawback to it is that I can't seem to look through it
without drooling...one time I went through and added up the "asking
prices" of all the vehicles in which I was interested...I stopped when
the figure passed 2 million.

David Boyd

Robert Merithew wants a good hand cleaner....

I recommend GOOP. It works for me. 

The stuff you put on before you work is called "Liquid Glove"
and it's available at most car parts places. It's basically
a latexy solution that you rub on and it dries and only feels
a little wierd....like a thin coat of gelatinous stuff on your
hands....once you're greasy and dirty, you'll never notice it.
But when you go to wash it off, the grease and ick just comes 
right off with soap and water.....assemble your car in the afternoon
and go shake hands with the pope that evening, no sweat.

Great stuff, both products are available at any major part palace.

Subject: 356/912 european heater swap

I recently located a good running 912 engine which has a european heater.
I have been looking for a spare engine (mostly as insurance against the
growing lack of availability of repair parts at reasonable prices) for
my 64 356 C.

The owner claims that the european 912 heater is practically a bolt-on:
all I would have to do is run hoses to my front heater boxes and discard the
existing linkage rod for the rear heater box flapper.  He said that the
European heater system is always "on", meaning that air from the blower
is always pressurizing the system, and that it does a much better job
of heating the car than the export heating system.

I seem to remember der Maestro saying not to attempt the 912 heater box
swap into a 356.  But, he may have been referring to the 912 export
heater system...  

Have any Porschephiles done this swap, or know the details of what
is required? 

BTW, the engine is priced $500 less without the heater sytem than
with.

thanks -

  Dave Erickson

I just use ordinary heavy duty hand cleaner paste, without pumice.  It takes
lots of scrubbing when you're done, but it always works.  You should NOT wet
your hands before using it, for maximum effectiveness.
