I gave the new luxury tax some thought, and don't have a problem with it.
If we all try to draw lines around things that we love to protect them from
taxes, pretty soon there's nothing left to tax. Now while I'm a big proponent
of the notion that "no government is good government", as long as we're gonna
have one, and it's gonna tax us, we might as well be equitable about it.

It's kinda like what my wife says about eating out "If you can't afford
to tip reasonably well, you can't afford to eat out." If I can afford
the car, I can afford the tax.

Stan

> But before I do that... I'd like to figure out some more "advanced"
> maneuvers. A friend of mine drives and restores old Chevy Z's... She
> told me about a method of slowing down smoothly called "heel and
> toing it" but this doesn't seem to be feasable with the pedal
> arrangement of the 944. Is this true or are my 10 1/2 feet too small?

I don't know what the pedal arrangement of the 944 is like, never having
driven one, but you can heel-and-toe in just about any car.  Not that it's
easy in every car, just that it can be done.  Sometimes you have to really
contort your foot, almost make it horizontal.  In an Acura Integra, the foot
is almost horizontal with the heel on the brake; in a Fiat X1/9 it's almost
horizontal with the heel on the throttle (and I personally find it somewhat
painful to hold my foot in that position).

It takes a lot of practice.  I have size 8AA feet, and if I can do it...

To: porsche@hpwarq.hp.com
Subject: feet too small


I am not familiar with the pedals on a 944. The 911/912/914/VW Beetle
layout, however, is the one that makes the term "heel and toe" actually
meaningful: you actually toe the brake pedal, and you actually heel
the gas. This is because the gas pedal goes right down to the floor,
where your heel can reach it.

Most "modern" cars don't work that way (THEY ARE WRONG (imho)), you
use the left side of your right foot for the brake, and the right
side for the gas. This is "not a good thing" because it means that
the tolerance for pedal activation height is much lower: the brakes
have to "come on" at about the same level at which the gas pedal
changes from "decelerate" to "accelerate", since (most people's)
feet are longer than they are wide.


-- 
---
John R. Lupien
lupienj@hpwarq.hp.com

I just attended my first PCA-sponsored 'drivers education' event at Road
Atlanta a couple of weeks ago. Absolutely exhilarating! $125 for two days (3
20-25 minute sessions each day). Had an instructor in the right seat each time
- great feedback. I knew immediately what I was doing right (and what I was
doing wrong). There's an incredible satisfaction when everything clicks and one
turn sets you up for the next... Hard to describe. I was really struck by the
difference in sensation a flat out acceleration on the track compares to on the
street. When my turbo kicks in on the street, it feels like someone is kicking
me in the pants - on the track, I almost always felt underpowered (mumbling to
myself "more, more, more..."). And this is pedal to the floor, full boost
acceleration, something I rarely if ever do on the street.

>But before I do that... I'd like to figure out some more "advanced"
>maneuvers. A friend of mine drives and restores old Chevy Z's... She
>told me about a method of slowing down smoothly called "heel and
>toing it" but this doesn't seem to be feasable with the pedal
>arrangement of the 944. Is this true or are my 10 1/2 feet too small?

I've been told that this is something you have to be shown how to do - it's
hard to describe. My instructor thought it best to concentrate on the basics
(driving the line, avoiding early apexes, avoiding engine braking, getting a
feel for how to control an insipient skid, getting confortable about driving
balls out into a turn etc.) before worrying about heal/toe
braking, etc.

>On a slightly different track... What do you guys have to say about
>the Porsche (and perhaps other) driving schools?

Join the PCA and drive as many events as you can (if you can't tell, I'm
addicted - I knew I was going to have fun, I just didn't realize _how much_
fun). I've heard that autocrossing is an excellent way to learn to control your
car at relatively low speed. I've not yet had an opportunity to attend one...

Again, I don't know about the pedal arrangement on a 944, but if I can
do it in a Golf (heel and toe, that is (-:), it can be done in ANY
car! You might have to do something like left-side-of-the-foot-
right-side instead of heel-and-toe, but it can be done. As John Lupien

The pedal arrangement is perfect.

On another note, and at the risk of starting a flame war, (but I know
that people on the mailing list are much more mature than the general
newsgroup readership, right?) it seems to me that the general
consensus is that heel-and-toe is NOT to be used as a means to slow
the car down. This is better left to the brakes. H-and-t should only
be used for smoother shifting (mainly downshifting). Anyone? Anyone?
Anyone?

>I just attended my first PCA-sponsored 'drivers education' event at Road
>Atlanta a couple of weeks ago. Absolutely exhilarating! $125 for two days (3
>20-25 minute sessions each day).
 
Track time is difficult to describe to someone who hasn't done it.  The
problem is that it is addicting, even if you're driving a car (quite an
admission from a motorcycle nut :-).
 
>(driving the line, avoiding early apexes, avoiding engine braking, getting a
                    ^^^^^^^^ ^^^^^ ^^^^^^
>feel for how to control an insipient skid, getting confortable about driving
>balls out into a turn etc.) before worrying about heal/toe
>braking, etc.
 
This is often very confuseing, and I think bikes and cars treat the problem
the same, but the terminology gets reversed sometimes.  I usually say that we
_try_ to get an early apex, that is to say, get slowed down early enough that
you do _most_ of your turning early in the corner and get back on the throttle
early.  A late apex is where you go wide on the exit because you overcooked
the entrance.  I think the confusion somes from whether people refer to the
bike (oops! vehicle) apex (the actual turning) or the _road_ apex (where the
vehicle comes closest to the inside edge).
 
Now, I maybe the one who has it backwards, and I'd like to hear other opinions
on the matter ("besides, what the h*ll does a motorcyclist know anyway?!?").
 
>Join the PCA and drive as many events as you can (if you can't tell, I'm
>addicted - I knew I was going to have fun, I just didn't realize _how much_
>fun). I've heard that autocrossing is an excellent way to learn to control your
>car at relatively low speed. I've not yet had an opportunity to attend one...
 
Auto-X is another excellent place to learn vehicle control.  It also has the
added advantage that if you screw-up, then all that happens is that you kill a
few cones (physical damage anyway, ego suicide is another story).
 
Just my opinion...
 
Al Bowers        bowers@elxsi.dfrf.nasa.gov

> On another note, and at the risk of starting a flame war, (but I know
> that people on the mailing list are much more mature than the general
> newsgroup readership, right?) it seems to me that the general
> consensus is that heel-and-toe is NOT to be used as a means to slow
> the car down. This is better left to the brakes. H-and-t should only
> be used for smoother shifting (mainly downshifting). 

Heel and toe is used for shifting, on that I will agree.
However, it is useless to pretend that the engine has
no role in slowing the car. The engine can profitably
be used to modify the brake bias forward or back, at need.
This is not usually important, but when it is, it can make
a big difference. For normal driving, using the brakes to
slow down makes a lot of sense, since it's a lot easier to
replace brake pads than engines.

On Nov 28,  2:31pm, Toufic Boubez writes:
> it seems to me that the general consensus is that heel-and-toe is NOT to be
> used as a means to slow the car down. This is better left to the brakes.
> H-and-t should only be used for smoother shifting (mainly downshifting).

To clarify the question somewhat: Not specifically heel-and-toe, but engine
braking, is what we don't want to use to slow down the car.

Right, the brakes were designed for slowing the car, and so they're the
proper thing to use to slow down.  Engine braking is not the root of all
evil, however.  It's just not that good for the engine (thought not
catastrophic), and certainly not nearly as effective as the brakes.

Right again, the purpose of heel-and-toe is to have the car already in the
proper gear for when you're ready to get off the brakes and back on the
throttle, smoothly.

To: bowers@elxsi.dfrf.nasa.gov, porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: re: Some discussions... Driving, etc...

> >(driving the line, avoiding early apexes, avoiding engine braking, getting a
>                     ^^^^^^^^ ^^^^^ ^^^^^^
> This is often very confuseing, and I think bikes and cars treat the problem
> the same, but the terminology gets reversed sometimes.  I usually say that we
> _try_ to get an early apex, that is to say, get slowed down early enough that
> you do _most_ of your turning early in the corner and get back on the throttle
> early.  A late apex is where you go wide on the exit because you overcooked
> the entrance.  I think the confusion somes from whether people refer to the
> bike (oops! vehicle) apex (the actual turning) or the _road_ apex (where the
> vehicle comes closest to the inside edge).
> Now, I maybe the one who has it backwards, and I'd like to hear other opinions
> on the matter ("besides, what the h*ll does a motorcyclist know anyway?!?").

The point at which you begin to turn is referred to (in most cases)
as the "turn-in point". The apex of a turn is where your vehicle
reaches its nearest point to the inside of the track (where your
knee guard catches grass, on a bike). If you over-cook the entry
to the turn, the closest you get to the inside of the track may be
your turn-in point (a very early apex indeed) and you either have to
make what should have been a single turn into several, or pick yourself
up out of the bushes. It is not always a bad idea to early apex: racing
situations can make it almost mandatory. The USGP of motorcycles last year
(I think it was last year) had the eventual winner going inside the 1st
and 2nd place runners at the same corner with two almost identical passes,
where he made the turn into two turns, taking the apex of the first
exactly as he got inside the other bike. A late apex is usually better
if there's nobody in the way, because it lets you make the last part
of the turn on the gas.



-- 
---
John R. Lupien
lupienj@hpwarq.hp.com

On Nov 28, 11:21am, bowers@elxsi.dfrf.nasa.gov writes:
> This is often very confuseing, ..., but the terminology gets reversed
> sometimes.  I usually say that we _try_ to get an early apex, that is to
> say, get slowed down early enough that you do _most_ of your turning early
> in the corner and get back on the throttle early.  A late apex is where you
> go wide on the exit because you overcooked the entrance.  I think the
> confusion somes from whether people refer to the bike (oops! vehicle) apex
> (the actual turning) or the _road_ apex (where the vehicle comes closest to
> the inside edge).
>  
> Now, I maybe the one who has it backwards, and I'd like to hear other
> opinions on the matter.

I think you are indeed the one who has it backwards, but your point is well
taken.  In every driving school I've taken, every book I've read, and with
most of the racers I associate with, the apex is the point (or area) at
which the vehicle is closest to the inside of the turn.

I have no comprehension of what you call the "vehicle apex"...  Maybe that's
when the vehicle is cornering at its hardest or something?  Maybe it's just
a bike term and that's why I don't understand it.

Now, the terms "early" and "late" are a little wierd.  In order to achieve a
late apex, you must turn later than for an early apex, yet this can be said
that you are setting up early.  Similarly, if you get behind ("late") in a
series of esses or in a slalom, it's because you're turning in too early
(apexing early).

Once again, to achieve a late apex, you set up early (staying wide and
turning in late), so that you can get on the throttle earlier.  It is, as
you say, somewhat confusing until it becomes second nature.

From: jims <ICOURIER/FINSERVER/jims%Softbridge@mcimail.com>

Subject: Heel and toe down-shifting
Message-Id: <90901128213409/0004275051NB1EM@mcimail.com>

I heartily agree that heel and toe operation is ONLY for smooth downshifting.
It is only used when you anticipate needing a lower gear, typically after
deceleration for a corner.

Downshifting should NEVER be used to slow a car!  This is a hold-over from
the early days of racing, when drum brakes weren't too good and it was OK to
sacrifice the machine to win the race.  Brake pads are cheaper than clutches!

Also, downshifting causes the driven wheels to act like weak brakes, and can
induce a spin on slippery roads.  I know a guy with a Z-28 [bash, bash] who
was going too fast and decided to slow by downshifting.  It was autumn in
New England, so he drove over wet leaves and the rear [braking] end swung
around. He went off the road and flipped the car.  Moral: just use the
brakes.

        JBS

Toufic wrote that H-and-T is used only for smoother shifting.  I think
what he meant is that h-and-t is used for smoother shifting while
braking.  I'm still a total novice at this but my assumptions were
that the h-and-t trick was used to match rev's so that the downshift
did not cause an added, and unexpected, braking force on the back
tires which could cause them to break loose.

With my old 944, I still used the brakes and the clutch to stop
especially on wet streets.  The idea was that I would brake until the
front tires broke loose (or just before) and then brake with the
engine (via the clutch) until the back wheels were about to break
loose.  Well, actually it was a game of breaking the front and then
the back and then the front by applying more and then less clutch.
This is just something I sorta thought up on my own and have no idea
if it is a valid technique.  Its not much different than "steering"
with the throttle alternating between understeer and oversteer.  It
seemed to work though.  The new ABS makes this trick unneeded for the
most part -- especially since I was only able to do it when I was
practicing and never really could do it when I really needed it.

All this aside, I've always wondered how good is a good race car
driver?  Do they use these tricks 99% of the time or not?  Also, I
often wonder how consistantly a good driver can recover from a spin.
Or do they simply know the limits and avoid the spins in the first
place.

pedz

On Nov 28,  2:44pm, Perry Smith writes:
> All this aside, I've always wondered how good is a good race car
> driver?  Do they use these tricks 99% of the time or not?  Also, I
> often wonder how consistantly a good driver can recover from a spin.
> Or do they simply know the limits and avoid the spins in the first
> place.

To oversimplify quite a bit, race drivers will always use threshhold braking
and heel-and-toe downshifts when a downshift is necessary.  To remove the
simplifications, sometimes they'll left-foot brake, sometimes they'll
downshift without the clutch, etc.  Sometimes, being human, they'll even
lockup a wheel or two while braking, miss a shift, etc.

Someone with lots of experience in catching spins will almost always be able
to catch a slow one, especially if you know it's coming because it's caused
by a mistake you made.  Practice makes perfect.  Racing means always driving
at the limit of adhesion - the idea is to stay right at the limit.  Again,
being human, this means that sometimes you're under it and sometimes you're
over it.  The fast way to drive a car on dry pavement is never to slide, but
catching spins is definitely part of the game.

Date: Wed, 28 Nov 90 22:03 GMT
From: jims <ICOURIER/FINSERVER/jims%Softbridge@mcimail.com>
To: Mark Sirota <msirota@ee.rochester.edu>
Cc: The Porsche Interest Group <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Subject: Heel and Toe downshifting, again
Message-Id: <15901128220351/0004275051NB4EM@mcimail.com>

Ok you guys, back to geometry class!  When we drive and talk about cornering,
we use the corner as the geometric reference and alter the vehicle's path to
"do the right thing".  The word "apex" refers to the corner, and specifically
the well known "inside" part of the turn.  That's what an apex is.  Imagine a
"V" drawn from the turn-in point, to the apex, and to the track-out point.
That's how the term's been used for the last umpteen years!  I can't imagine
why another motor vehicle group might use another interpretation!  This is
sounding like religion...

I do use engine braking in one circumstance: when I'm coasting to a
stop.  In a Motronic car, a closed throttle and RPM over about 1200 shuts the
fuel flow off completely.  This saves gas and helps drop engine temperature
for a moment.  If you do this before exit ramps on highways, just think of
all the brake wear and gas you'll save!  Unless, of course, you're like me
and see every off-ramp as just another challenge...

Aside from the above use, which is really just "coasting" and not
downshifting to decelerate, I'd never use engine braking.  Balancing the
brake bias with it is a scary thought - it's much too hard to control to the
precise level needed, and you can only _increase_ braking at the drive axle,
not the other (front) end where 2/3 of the braking force is.  If the brakes
are so out of balance that this trick actually helps, then get a bias
adjusting valve!

        JBS

speed onto the next straight.  So being inefficient in the first corner of
series is _usuallY- a good thing, provided it places you in the correct place
for the exit of the last corner.

>of the turn on the gas.
 
Now both Mark and John say that the `apex' is defined as the point where the
vehicle track approaches the inside of the corner the closest.  Bike racers
call this a `false' apex or a `track' apex.  (I'm trying to explain this the
way it was taught to me, I'll get my references tomorrow.)  If there were
infinite asphalt everywhere, then this definition has no meaning, even if the
turn is geometrically the same.  So then the mathmatical definition of `apex'
is where the turn radius is the smallest (or absolute value of the 2nd
derivative is largest).  This point in the vehicle path occurs long before the
inside edge of the track is approached.  Now you say, "but the track is
causing the path to be such".  True, but any number of conditions can make
that particular path the optimum.  Once the optimum path is selected, ignore
the track, stick with your own game plan.
 
The track schools where I have heard this were:
 
California Superbike School
Kawasaki's `Ticket to Ride'
American Federation of Motorcyclists
 
And the references I offer are (I'll check on these tonight):
 
Motorcycle Road Racing: Art and Science
A Twist of the Wrist
Soft Science of Motorcycle Road Racing
A Canyon Racers Guide to Fast Street Riding
 
So _I_ believe that we are saying the same thing.  I just think we call it
something different.
 
But then, just my opinion...

From porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu  Wed Nov 28 17:49:26 1990

Received: from SUN.COM by bcm.tmc.edu (AA03049); Wed, 28 Nov 90 17:21:15 CST

Received: from formula1.Corp.Sun.COM by Corp.Sun.COM (4.1/SMI-4.1)
	id AA17943; Wed, 28 Nov 90 15:21:09 PST
Received: by formula1.Corp.Sun.COM (4.0/SMI-4.0)
	id AA01483; Wed, 28 Nov 90 15:18:34 PST
Date: Wed, 28 Nov 90 15:18:34 PST
From: sai@corp.sun.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs [Wordsmith])
Message-Id: <9011282318.AA01483@formula1.Corp.Sun.COM>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Re:  Heel and Toe downshifting, again

> Ok you guys, back to geometry class!  When we drive and talk about cornering,
> we use the corner as the geometric reference and alter the vehicle's path to
> "do the right thing".  The word "apex" refers to the corner, and specifically
> the well known "inside" part of the turn.  That's what an apex is.  Imagine a
> "V" drawn from the turn-in point, to the apex, and to the track-out point.
> That's how the term's been used for the last umpteen years!  I can't imagine
> why another motor vehicle group might use another interpretation! 

 That's because you're being car-centric. In motorcycle racing,
 the term apex refers to the point at which you flick the
 bike over and begin your drive out of the corner. Given that
 with a bike there are many more lines through the corner,
 I can see how car drivers could become confused at the difference
 in terminology.

 When we speak of "late-apexing" a corner it's because there
 are two general forms of taking a turn in use in motorcycle
 racing right now. There is the "late apex" (in which the
 turn taken by the bike follows a parabolic arc), where you continue
 straight for longer, braking harder, and then flick it over
 and drive out of the corner (often clipping the interior
 apex of the turn on the drive out) and then there is the
 "classic line", where you ride the turn in more of an arc.
 
 Either turn is valid. One often uses the late apex to pass
 a rider taking a classic line. The classic line generally
 carries more speed through the turn but this is offset by
 the fact that you can't use the speed if someone appears
 in front of you (having taken the late apex line).

 In general, I rarely "apex" the corner the way a car
 driver would. Rather, I clip with a knee as I drive out
 (so I guess we generally apex our turns sooner).

 So be careful in making generalizations and realize that
 there are two apexes in any turn. The one on the tarmac
 and the one from your turn. The two do not necessarily
 coincide.

 Stefan
 (who races formula motorcycles and will autox very soon now)

I suppose I should add my two cents worth...

The apex is to the best of my knowledge, the "ideal" spot at which you should
come closest to the inside of a corner.  That doesn't always mean getting on to
the turtles, of course.  Depends on the corner.  Some corners, those that go
through a larger change in compass direction, will require a double apex.  (Many
highway ramps are this way.  Big Bend at Lime Rock is a double.)

So, a "late" or "early" apex really is a someone's way of telling you that you
did it differently.  Depending on your car, this ideal spot may or may not be a
good thing.  911's handle differently that 944's, we are told.  Us learners
should generally stick to the turn in, apex, and track out points as laid out by
those benevolent souls who showed up real early that morning and set up the
cones!  Once you progress (or if you have an opinionated instructor), you'll
probably want to try different apexes and lines.

Since cones are noticibly absent in real races, it makes sense to start finding
reference points that aren't orange and triangular.  The three pylons really
aren't enough, anyways.  Pavement aprons and transitions work well for me, as do
mysterious splotches of white paint.  You used to head straight for the
restrooms coming out of Big Bend and going into the esses at LRP, for example. 
There was (maybe still is) a splotch of white paint in the left hander in the
esses.

Heel-toeing is how you make two feet do three things.  The one with the big "L"
sewn over the toes works the clutch, and the one with the big "R" works the
brake ('cause you want to slow down) and the gas ('cause you want to rev match
while downshifting).  The idea is that, after braking and come time to get on
the gas, you don't find you're turning 1800 RPM's.  By downshifting, you pace
yourself at the right point in your car's torque curve to come out of the corner
strong.  If your car is a turbo, this also keeps the turbocharger spooled up and
on boost.

You don't learn heel-toe at the track or autocross.  You learn it on the street
and bring it with you.  If you're uncomfortable doing it, don't.  It won't buy
you much.  Pick a gear you're comfortable in, and stay in it (third is nice). 
You have more than enough to keep your adrenalin-soaked noggin occupied anyways.
(For example, Senna stuffed his car at one of the last F1's this year when he
missed a shift.)

I have size 8 feet, and I find the foot-vertical technique doesn't work well for
me.  I am changing now to try using the front of the foot on the brake, the heel
on the gas.  I am finding that I really have to centre my instep over the brake
pedal, then swing my heel out to the gas, to get the right action.  Anyone have
similar experiences?

Yes, you can downshift to slow the car down.  In the halcyon days of drum
brakes, people used to do this a lot more in order to save the brakes. 
Fittipaldi tried this last year when he had brake problems, and ended up
trashing his transmission and DNF'ing.  (Don't these big-time examples help?) 
Nowadays, it's best to save your transmission and use the pads.  I will
down-shift to slow myself or just maintain speed cruising down a hill in street
driving.  It takes a long time, at least for me, to be very smooth at all speeds
and rev's, and I practice as much as possible.

As others have mentioned, PCA autocrosses and track events are tons of fun and
very cheap.  The professional schools are great too.  They're expensive, but the
education you get, I am told, is worth it.

Well over the allotted two cents...

Kevin Gross

We're all talking about the particulars of a corner but using an undefined
term.  The car folks talk only about the turn's geometry, and ignore the line
the car takes.  The bikers DO consider the line the bike takes, and use the
word "apex" in reference to that line.  This is independant of the corner
itself, and would remain valid on the Salt Flats.

George Treacy writes:

>I plan to rebuild the engine pretty much to stock with the exception of
>junking the fuel-injection  and replacing it with either Weber of Dellorto
>carbs.

I did that on my 1971 1.7 using twin Webers. It might be subjective,
(I have no hard timing data), but I really like the results. The
engine seems to breathe and respond better in both city and highway
driving. The only drawback is in-town traffic jam driveability, where
you can feel he carbs stumbling :-), but I'm sure this can be
remedied by some appropriate tuning. I just never bothered to do it (I
rarely used my 914 for commuting, it was mainly for fun and trips -
that's one of the advantages of living in a nice mass transportation-
minded city like Montreal - plug plug).

Also, you might want to look into replacing the stock muffler with
something that breathes better (I put a Monza muffler, but I'm not
sure I'd recommend them. Although I think they DO provide a slight
power increase, the workmanship is pretty lousy, and parts of the
tubing rusted within 6 months).

Well, that's my 5 cents' worth (inflation). Good luck, and most of
all, HAVE FUN! Let us know what happens.

toufic

Subject: Re: 914 FI-carb mods.

 Most everybody knowledgeable I've talked to has
 recommended against replacing the fuel injection
 on a stock motor with carbs (for a 914). Apparently,
 you get really bad off-idle performance and
 low-speed driveability. If your car is a daily
 driver it probably won't be worth it. Aside from
 the cost, the fuel injection system is relatively
 trouble free with a little maintenance. 

 If you're building the motor, you might consider
 one of the programmable fuel injection systems.
 I don't think it would be an overstatement to
 say that fuel injection will give you superior
 performance over carburetion in every instance
 (when properly implemented). I believe the
 control is just much more precise. 

 IMHO.

 Stefan

Actually, Richard, you can use e-mail in a one-way sense to talk to the
suppliers.  Most commercial e-mail systems allow sending a FAX.  For example,
MCI Mail, MHS (eg, DaVinci), cc:mail, and many others support this.  So you
could send in your order from your mailer.

A nice thing about this is that the received FAX is of really high print
quality, because there is no alignment problem.  MCI Mail offers you the
additional ability to have recorded your company's logo or your scanned
signature for blitting onto the FAX.

This may be one of the few advantages to not being directly on the Internet!

Kevin Gross
